  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a3c06292-f67a-4348-b6e5-286cfdcd7372'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<div id="review_1500839201" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1500839201" />
    <a title="Katriina ❆" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13511873-katriina"><img alt="Katriina ❆" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546803914p2/13511873.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1500839201?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katriina ❆" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katriina ❆" href="/user/show/13511873-katriina">Katriina ❆</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13511873-katriina?shelf=in-english">in-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13511873-katriina?shelf=in-original-language">in-original-language</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1500839201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12495934924543399257">I enjoyed this little tale a lot! <br/><br/>When I was a young girl, Alice in Wonderland was probably my favourite modern fairy tale. I watched so many adaptions, from really old Disney cartoons to the modern Tim Burton adaption with my favourite actress Helena Bonham Carter, and I even participated in a play that was based on Alice in Wonderland. <br/>However, to this day, I have never read the actual book by Lewis Carrol. I only knew the plot from the movie adaptions and the theatre script, and was amazed to</span>
  <span id="freeText12495934924543399257" style="display:none">I enjoyed this little tale a lot! <br /><br />When I was a young girl, Alice in Wonderland was probably my favourite modern fairy tale. I watched so many adaptions, from really old Disney cartoons to the modern Tim Burton adaption with my favourite actress Helena Bonham Carter, and I even participated in a play that was based on Alice in Wonderland. <br />However, to this day, I have never read the actual book by Lewis Carrol. I only knew the plot from the movie adaptions and the theatre script, and was amazed to find out how much they all left out. <br /><br />Alice in Wonderland is so, so much more than the Mad Hatter, the March Hare and the Cheshire Cat. That is what I found out, after all of these years, and I'm so glad I decided to read the book for elsewise I would've never known these grandious characters. <br /><br />Now, especially in the Tim Burton Movie they added a lot of the sequel, <i>Through the looking glass and what Alice found there</i>, into the movie and left out lot of characters that might have been so minor, but they were so lovely in the actual tale! <br /><br />I adored the pigeon that was looking for serpents and mistook Alice as one, the sad, sad Mock Turtle and the cute, sleepy little dormouse. I might also be slightly biased because I adooore pigeons and turtles, but really, they were great characters. And so was the duchess with her morals, that would have been something I would have certainly missed out on. <br /><br />The writing style was OK I'd say. I mean I don't know whether it was good for the standards of that time, but I found it to be a little too descriptive, although there weren't too many unnecessary descriptions in the book. The dialogues and the characters of the animals were grandious though, and I chuckled every here and there.<br /><br />Personally, I thought this was a great book, but nothing too special in writing. Furthermore, I am afraid that having seen so many adaptions has kind of killed my joy about the plot, because if I wouldn't have known about Alice in Wonderland before, I would have been able to enjoy this much, much more. Anyway. It was a solid three star book and I have absolutely nothing to complan about. <br /><br />By the way, there is a second movie coming out, again starring brilliant Helena Bonham Carter and magnificent Johnny Depp. I so love these two, especially when they team up with Tim Burton. I can hardly wait! </span>
  <a data-text-id="12495934924543399257" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1029823801" />
    <a title="Georgie Hacon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29529440-georgie-hacon"><img alt="Georgie Hacon" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1029823801?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Georgie Hacon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Georgie Hacon" href="/user/show/29529440-georgie-hacon">Georgie Hacon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1029823801" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10824389075340585292">Curiouser and curiouser...Why does everyone seem to hate Alice?? Alice in Wonderland is a bizarre story, but what it gives is a sense of adventure and something I live by; "The impossible can be possible - you only have to imagine it." Alice has had such a strong influence on hundreds, if not thousands, of people who can relate to this bumbling, curious, imaginative little girl. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

          </span>

<div id="review_1113261589" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1113261589" />
    <a title="Megan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35576858-megan"><img alt="Megan" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1113261589?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan" href="/user/show/35576858-megan">Megan</a>
      </span>

          added it



          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Ages between 10-16</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1113261589" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16874253930704269743">In Alice in Wonderland by Lewis Carroll, the story line is simply complex. Basically, a young girl named Alice falls into a rabbit hole. Alice travels through the rabbit hole to magical world known as Wonderland. Wonderland is full of unreal creatures. Alice encounters a white rabbit, mad hatter, talking flowers, a large caterpillar, the Cheshire Cat and much more. Alice also means the Queen of Hearts. The Queen of Hearts sends Alice with the Gryphon as her escort to meet the Mock Turtle. Alice </span>
  <span id="freeText16874253930704269743" style="display:none">In Alice in Wonderland by Lewis Carroll, the story line is simply complex. Basically, a young girl named Alice falls into a rabbit hole. Alice travels through the rabbit hole to magical world known as Wonderland. Wonderland is full of unreal creatures. Alice encounters a white rabbit, mad hatter, talking flowers, a large caterpillar, the Cheshire Cat and much more. Alice also means the Queen of Hearts. The Queen of Hearts sends Alice with the Gryphon as her escort to meet the Mock Turtle. Alice shares her interesting experiences with the Mock Turtle and the Gryphon, they listen to the weirdness of her adventures. Alice insults the King of Hearts, and the Queen becomes furious with Alice and orders her beheading, but Alice grows to a huge size and knocks over the Queen’s army of playing cards. Alice suddenly wakes up, realizing it was only a dream. Through the plot events, we see the author’s message that everyone is different in their own way. We understand this theme through events such as Alice thinking that she was going insane from boredom, just before she entered Wonderland. An important quote from the book that reinforces the theme is “We're all mad here.” This is important because it shows that in Wonderland, everyone is crazy in their own way, and that it is okay to be different. <br />This book was very interesting for me because I feel like Alice and I have the same mind. The slightest things bore us, but also the slightest things amuse us. This book showed me that there are places in the world where everyone will be accepted for themselves. Alice in Wonderland relates to me because I often have very vivid, elaborate dreams and imaginations. After reading the last page of Alice in Wonderland, the reader is left with the feeling that there is a place for you in this world. As humans, we should take the advice of the author which is be yourself. I believe this book is worth our time because it really puts things into a different perspective. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16874253930704269743" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14757435428160614932">Alice in Wonderland by Lewis Carroll made me thing about people's major insecurities and doubts in life, the author represents this trough Alice, the protagonist, that would represent ourselves and her adventures in wonderland that would be the life.<br/>Alice, when she first felt into the rabbit's hole, she found a little door where she wanted to pass and a magic bottle; if you drink it you'll grow bigger or smaller, she tried several times to get the correct size but she couldn't, so she get frustr</span>
  <span id="freeText14757435428160614932" style="display:none">Alice in Wonderland by Lewis Carroll made me thing about people's major insecurities and doubts in life, the author represents this trough Alice, the protagonist, that would represent ourselves and her adventures in wonderland that would be the life.<br />Alice, when she first felt into the rabbit's hole, she found a little door where she wanted to pass and a magic bottle; if you drink it you'll grow bigger or smaller, she tried several times to get the correct size but she couldn't, so she get frustrated and sad, just like people in real life when they can't get what they want despite their efforts. "And when she had tired herself out with trying, the poor little thing sat down and cried". After all her tries to pass trough the door and get the perfect size drinking from the magic bottle, she just sat down and cried.<br />farther in the book, Alice founds a giant caterpillar, smoking from a hooka, and this caterpillar asked Alice the question 'who are you? and Alice couldn't find the answer to this question because she couldn't realize if she was still herself or other person. Find our real identity is hard, sometimes you don't know yourself enough, like Alice in the book. "Who are you?' said the caterpillar...Alice replied, rather shyly, 'I-I hardly know sir, just at present- at least I know who I was when I get up this morning." Alice can't respond to the caterpillar question because she can't find her identity."<br />This book represent peoples insecurities and doubts about life in a animated and distortionated way.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11133814961577742983">Alice in Wonderland I think defines the phrase 'timeless classic'. The story for me never ever gets old and I think should be in every teacher's cupboard at some point in their career! Everything about the book is just wonderful and what happens could only ever happen in a dream! Where imagination meets reality for Alice, anything is possible - and i think this message can infuse children's writing and is to me a central building block for writing within children's stories. Letting them know tha</span>
  <span id="freeText11133814961577742983" style="display:none">Alice in Wonderland I think defines the phrase 'timeless classic'. The story for me never ever gets old and I think should be in every teacher's cupboard at some point in their career! Everything about the book is just wonderful and what happens could only ever happen in a dream! Where imagination meets reality for Alice, anything is possible - and i think this message can infuse children's writing and is to me a central building block for writing within children's stories. Letting them know that the absurd can be incredible within writing - that there should be no boundaries, no restraints to ones writing, and that the more absurd it sounds, the more believable within your story it becomes. <br /><br />It is truly wonderful - and the scenes that follow each other just get better and better. Lewis Carroll's writing and descriptions are amazing - and it leaves you with a real sense of feeling like you know the characters, the emotions, their mannerisms, how they would talk etc. Due to this I was a little let down by the on screen Tim Burton adaptation and wasn't quite what I invisedged after reading the book - and due to this I would say to please read the book and it might give you a completely different outlook on the characters. Read the book first ( not the other way round as many seem to do!) and let that form your opinion as I think many may not read the book after they have watched it on screen.<br /><br />The characters in Alice and Wonderland are imaginative and the story is fun and puzzling. All the different characters from Alice to the Mad Hatter , the White Rabbit and The Caterpillar all come together to make a truly wonderful timeless classic that I hope will be enjoyed by generations to come!<br /><br />Favourite Quote :<br />“Would you tell me, please, which way I ought to go from here?"<br />"That depends a good deal on where you want to get to."<br />"I don't much care where –"<br />"Then it doesn't matter which way you go.” <br />― Lewis Carroll, Alice in Wonderland</span>

<div id="review_1081644963" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1081644963" />
    <a title="YingTing" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35996483-yingting"><img alt="YingTing" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1081644963?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YingTing" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YingTing" href="/user/show/35996483-yingting">YingTing</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1081644963&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1081644963" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2349117183494712505">Reading Alice in Wonderland is a choice I will never regret; it was most definitely one of the best books I have ever read, and forgetting about it will be near impossible. <br/><br/>	Throughout this book, there are many noticeable aspects readers will definitely notice. As a reader progresses through Alice in Wonderland, one will most likely notice the author’s way of crafting.<br/><br/>	In the novel, the rabbit hole symbolizes the curiosity of the protagonist, also known as Alice. Through this rabbit hole, Alice </span>
  <span id="freeText2349117183494712505" style="display:none">Reading Alice in Wonderland is a choice I will never regret; it was most definitely one of the best books I have ever read, and forgetting about it will be near impossible. <br /><br />	Throughout this book, there are many noticeable aspects readers will definitely notice. As a reader progresses through Alice in Wonderland, one will most likely notice the author’s way of crafting.<br /><br />	In the novel, the rabbit hole symbolizes the curiosity of the protagonist, also known as Alice. Through this rabbit hole, Alice shows many signs of curiosity, starting from the fact that she could not hold herself back, straight-on diving headfirst into the hole, as curiosity had gotten the best of her. As a reader, I also believe that the rabbit hole may symbolize crossroads. Firstly, Alice must cross the rabbit hole in order to arrive at the alternate reality of Wonderland. Secondly, when the rabbit hole is crossed, she is faced with endless challenges, decisions and opportunities, much like what a crossroad does to one in real life. After crossing, you are then faced with additional problems.<br /><br />	Throughout the novel, Carroll has used various forms of literary techniques to communicate the book’s concepts; for instance, the techniques used may include oxymoron, hyperbole and so on.<br />	<br />	Readers can clearly see the use of oxymoron in the novel. An example can be found numerous times in Alice in Wonderland. “…and, as the Lory positively refused to tell its age, there was no more to be said” (32). In this example, the words “positively refused” come together to create an oxymoron. When somebody refuses something, he or she feels negative, in regards to the situation. Thus, when the two words, “positively refuses”, are used together, “positive” and “refuse” contradict each other, creating an oxymoron.<br /><br />	With the oxymorons used by Carroll, readers can look at the novel from a different perspective. Usage of the oxymoron helps people look at words from a different angle, as it can describe matters with a completely new approach. <br /><br />	Lastly, another literary technique, the hyperbole, is found in Alice in Wonderland numerous times. “The table was a large one, but the three were all crowded together at one corner of it. ‘No room! No room!’ they cried out when they saw Alice coming” (70).In this example, “the three” (which consists of the Mad Hatter, March Hare and Dormouse, other figures from the novel) had said there was “no” room in a large table of which they were only taking up a small portion of. They had exaggerated the space left on the table so that there appeared to be none left, when there was, in reality, far from no space. <br /><br />	The author’s use of hyperbole puts light on the various situations in the story. Readers, through seeing the techniques used, can now look at the story from a slightly more humorous perspective. <br /><br />	Throughout the novel, readers can also see the literary elements the author has made use of, such as introducing the characters and setting the mood of each aspect of the story. The author effectively communicates ideas of every part of the novel through giving readers insight to various elements of the story. <br />	<br />	In Alice in Wonderland, Carroll has introduced all characters effectively without fail. An example of one character that was described particularly well is the Mad Hatter. Firstly, the author has given the character such a name that describes his [the character’s] personality. The Mad Hatter is, indeed, rather mad. Throughout the story, he is described to behave and think in a queer fashion. Examples of his strange behavior in the novel could include various scenes from the Mad Tea-Party. <br />	<br />	During the Mad Hatter’s appearances, he seems to often have random thoughts that do not relate to each other at all whatsoever. An example of his queer ways of thinking is found in the midst of the novel. “…while Alice thought over all she could remember about ravens and writing-desks, which wasn’t much. The Hatter was the first to break the silence. ‘What day of the Month is it?’” (71). This shows that the Hatter does have strange thoughts, as one does not usually transition from the topic of ravens and writing-desks to days of months, because of the fact that they are not very much related to the other.<br /><br />	Another character that has been well-described is Alice. Being the protagonist, this character is likely to have been described most effectively. From the beginning, all readers can clearly see Alice has a curious mind, and loves to discover more about everything and everyone. She is also shown to often speak her mind and be straightforward about her thoughts. For instance, she has offended the mouse on various occasions, as she had not thought about the mouse’s fear of cats. When she simply spoke what she thought about her cat, Dinah, to the mouse, she had accidentally offended him.<br /><br />	The author also describes settings in the novel effectively, so that readers can picture events with ease. With numerous examples of descriptive language, Carroll describes settings in such a way that makes readers feel as if they are right there, next to the characters. Throughout the novel, readers can easily see the absurd, unreal settings.<br /><br />	Another aspect of the crafting that should be pointed out is the author’s skill of describing and establishing the various moods of the story. When one is introduced to Wonderland, any reader would see the oddness of settings in the novel with ease, as the moods described would bounce back and forth.<br /><br />	Overall, Lewis Carroll seems to write in a boisterous, fun mood. One can see so, from the various examples in his work, as Carroll often writes in uneven parts; some writing might be in short and long sentences. Also, he must have a rather crazy style of thought, as the animals are talking, and such a place as Wonderland even exists.<br /><br />	Ultimately, it is definitely a favorite book of mine. Finding another novel which is as great and unique as Alice in Wonderland is out of the question. A book like this will always last in any reader’s mind, and will forever remain with generations to come.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2349117183494712505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7963871013855474327">Alice in Wonderland is one of my favorite books. It is also my mom's favorite and she was who made me fall in love with it through books and movies that are also beautiful.<br/>I think it is one of the books that I have reread the most ... I just love it! The puns, meaningless riddles and the simple madness and ridicules of its pages make it an entertaining and absolutely captivating read. Little Alice's emotional and mental intelligence together with the occurrences of all the characters make the st</span>
  <span id="freeText7963871013855474327" style="display:none">Alice in Wonderland is one of my favorite books. It is also my mom's favorite and she was who made me fall in love with it through books and movies that are also beautiful.<br />I think it is one of the books that I have reread the most ... I just love it! The puns, meaningless riddles and the simple madness and ridicules of its pages make it an entertaining and absolutely captivating read. Little Alice's emotional and mental intelligence together with the occurrences of all the characters make the story completely catch the reader.<br />Although it was designed as a book to entertain little girls, I think it has a degree of difficulty and a certain wealth of rhetorical figures that make it more suitable for adults.<br />It is one of those stories that can be enjoyed at any age and is never boring. It also appeals to the child inside us and I think it is an excellent option to feel happy and have a light reading.<br />At home we have some editions (6 actually) and it's fun for me to compare the translations and illustrations of all of them.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3cDVGa05j4wygaVh5wTPFHnOv9W9u0PMUwyvcvg3iEidUos8N8eeRNcDHla15ddezeTQCbnFD/5UY4xdC3elIw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

She encounters a dizzying array of extraordinary characters that include talking flowers, Kings and Queens, Tweedledee and Tweedledum and Humpty Dumpty himself. This brand new stage adaptation by one of Canada\'s most beloved authors and playwrights was a feature production of the 1994 Stratford Festival season. His poems combine \'karumi\', or lightness of touch, with the Zen ideal of oneness with creation. Each poem evokes the natural world - the cherry blossom, the leaping frog, the summer moon or the winter snow - suggesting the smallness of human life in comparison to the vastness and drama of nature. Basho himself enjoyed solitude and a life free from possessions, and his haiku are the work of an observant eye and a meditative mind, uncluttered by materialism and alive to the beauty of the world This partial loads on almost every page view.  The associated React component makes
a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.
This is determined by how many signed out pagehits the user has executed an how recently they have
last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
